  <title>Time of day and network reprogramming during drought induced CAM photosynthesis in Sedum album</title>

  <link rel="canonical" href="https://journals.plos.org/plosgenetics/article?id=10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" />
  <meta name="description" content="Author summary Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) photosynthesis represents an important adaptation to arid environments as CAM plants take up CO2 at night when evapotranspiration rates are lower. Genomes and large-scale datasets are available for several plants with constitutive CAM activity, but they provided little insight on how this trait evolved from C3. Here we sequenced the CAM-cycling plant Sedum album, which switches from C3 to CAM photosynthesis under drought conditions. We performed a global gene expression analysis sampling every two hours over 24 hours in C3 (well-watered) and CAM (drought) conditions. This comparative approach allowed us to identify components of the CAM pathway that were previously unidentified in constitutive CAM plants such as pineapple and orchid. Our results reveal a massive time of day specific rewiring of the transcriptional networks in Sedum where only 20% of cycling genes overlap between the C3 and CAM conditions. This time of day reprogramming results in broad network changes linking stress pathways and photosynthesis under CAM." />

  <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Plants with facultative crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) maximize performance through utilizing C3 or C4 photosynthesis under ideal conditions while temporally switching to CAM under water stress (drought). While genome-scale analyses of constitutive CAM plants suggest that time of day networks are shifted, or phased to the evening compared to C3, little is known for how the shift from C3 to CAM networks is modulated in drought induced CAM. Here we generate a draft genome for the drought-induced CAM-cycling species Sedum album. Through parallel sampling in well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM) conditions, we uncover a massive rewiring of time of day expression and a CAM and stress-specific network. The core circadian genes are expanded in S. album and under CAM induction, core clock genes either change phase or amplitude. While the core clock cis-elements are conserved in S. album, we uncover a set of novel CAM and stress specific cis-elements consistent with our finding of rewired co-expression networks. We identified shared elements between constitutive CAM and CAM-cycling species and expression patterns unique to CAM-cycling S. album. Together these results demonstrate that drought induced CAM-cycling photosynthesis evolved through the mobilization of a stress-specific, time of day network, and not solely the phasing of existing C3 networks. These results will inform efforts to engineer water use efficiency into crop plants for growth on marginal land.">


  <meta name="keywords" content="Starches,Genomics,Gene expression,C3 photosynthesis,Circadian rhythms,Genetic networks,Tetraploidy,Circadian oscillators" />

        <div class="article-text" id="artText">
          



<div class="abstract toc-section abstract-type-"><a id="abstract0" name="abstract0" data-toc="abstract0" class="link-target" title="Abstract"></a><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="abstract-content"><a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Plants with facultative crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) maximize performance through utilizing C3 or C4 photosynthesis under ideal conditions while temporally switching to CAM under water stress (drought). While genome-scale analyses of constitutive CAM plants suggest that time of day networks are shifted, or phased to the evening compared to C3, little is known for how the shift from C3 to CAM networks is modulated in drought induced CAM. Here we generate a draft genome for the drought-induced CAM-cycling species <em>Sedum album</em>. Through parallel sampling in well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM) conditions, we uncover a massive rewiring of time of day expression and a CAM and stress-specific network. The core circadian genes are expanded in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and under CAM induction, core clock genes either change phase or amplitude. While the core clock <em>cis</em>-elements are conserved in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, we uncover a set of novel CAM and stress specific <em>cis</em>-elements consistent with our finding of rewired co-expression networks. We identified shared elements between constitutive CAM and CAM-cycling species and expression patterns unique to CAM-cycling <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. Together these results demonstrate that drought induced CAM-cycling photosynthesis evolved through the mobilization of a stress-specific, time of day network, and not solely the phasing of existing C3 networks. These results will inform efforts to engineer water use efficiency into crop plants for growth on marginal land.</p>
</div></div><div class="abstract toc-section abstract-type-summary"><a id="abstract1" name="abstract1" data-toc="abstract1" class="link-target" title="Author summary"></a>
<h2>Author summary</h2>
<div class="abstract-content"><a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract2.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) photosynthesis represents an important adaptation to arid environments as CAM plants take up CO<sub>2</sub> at night when evapotranspiration rates are lower. Genomes and large-scale datasets are available for several plants with constitutive CAM activity, but they provided little insight on how this trait evolved from C3. Here we sequenced the CAM-cycling plant <em>Sedum album</em>, which switches from C3 to CAM photosynthesis under drought conditions. We performed a global gene expression analysis sampling every two hours over 24 hours in C3 (well-watered) and CAM (drought) conditions. This comparative approach allowed us to identify components of the CAM pathway that were previously unidentified in constitutive CAM plants such as pineapple and orchid. Our results reveal a massive time of day specific rewiring of the transcriptional networks in Sedum where only 20% of cycling genes overlap between the C3 and CAM conditions. This time of day reprogramming results in broad network changes linking stress pathways and photosynthesis under CAM.</p>
</div></div>


<div class="articleinfo"><p><strong>Citation: </strong>Wai CM, Weise SE, Ozersky P, Mockler TC, Michael TP, VanBuren R (2019) Time of day and network reprogramming during drought induced CAM photosynthesis in <em>Sedum album</em>. PLoS Genet 15(6):
           e1008209.

RNAseq raw data were deposited under BioProject PRJNA487770. Assembled genomes and annotations are also available on CoGe. The following RNAseq based data files can be found on CoGe: README files (<a href="https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/51CA6F8B-F176-4570-9FBE-24C599A31353/README.txt">https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/51CA6F8B-F176-4570-9FBE-24C599A31353/README.txt</a>), Orthogroup result (<a href="https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/CF32EA69-38A9-4D9E-8D2E-2CE2974FE559/Sedum_OrthoFinder_Orthogroups.txt">https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/CF32EA69-38A9-4D9E-8D2E-2CE2974FE559/Sedum_OrthoFinder_Orthogroups.txt</a>), JTK result (<a href="https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/10E4F0DE-F2BC-4302-86CA-ED4B5824E104/Sedum_RNAseq_JTK_results.txt">https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/10E4F0DE-F2BC-4302-86CA-ED4B5824E104/Sedum_RNAseq_JTK_results.txt</a>), WGCNA results (<a href="https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/511FF841-B948-4CE2-8C28-D6EB7BC7FB58/Sedum_RNAseq_WGCNA_results.txt">https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/511FF841-B948-4CE2-8C28-D6EB7BC7FB58/Sedum_RNAseq_WGCNA_results.txt</a>), RNAseq expression table using the genome V2 (<a href="https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/70B54402-6696-4810-BBE4-B6492A5A666E/Sedum_RNAseq_expression_table-V2.txt">https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/70B54402-6696-4810-BBE4-B6492A5A666E/Sedum_RNAseq_expression_table-V2.txt</a>) and RNAseq expression table using the genome V3 (<a href="https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/70B54402-6696-4810-BBE4-B6492A5A666E/Sedum_RNAseq_expression_table-V2.txt">https://de.cyverse.org/dl/d/70B54402-6696-4810-BBE4-B6492A5A666E/Sedum_RNAseq_expression_table-V2.txt</a>).</p><p><strong>Funding: </strong>This work is funded by the National Science Foundation (IOS 1401572 to RV; <a href="http://www.nsf.gov">www.nsf.gov</a>). <div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec001" name="sec001" data-toc="sec001" class="link-target" title="Introduction"></a><h2>Introduction</h2><a id="article1.body1.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Drought is the most pervasive abiotic stress and plants have evolved diverse strategies to mitigate the effects of water deficit [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref001" class="ref-tip">1</a>]. Most water loss in plants occurs through transpiration as a byproduct of daytime stomata mediated CO<sub>2</sub> uptake. Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) plants have evolved an alternative carbon assimilation pathway to store CO<sub>2</sub> nocturnally when evapotranspiration rates are lower [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref002" class="ref-tip">2</a>]. CAM plants store and concentrate atmospheric or respiratory CO<sub>2</sub> at night through the carboxylation of phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP) by the enzyme phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PPC). The resulting four carbon acid, oxaloacetate, is subsequently reduced to malate by malate dehydrogenase (MDH), and then transported to the vacuole as malic acid, producing the characteristic nighttime acidification observed in CAM plants [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref003" class="ref-tip">3</a>]. During the day, malic acid is decarboxylated to release the CO<sub>2</sub> for fixation by Rubisco. Because of this temporal separation, CAM plants have remarkably high water use efficiency, and use roughly 35% less water than C4 plants and up to 80% less water than comparable C3 species [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref004" class="ref-tip">4</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref005" class="ref-tip">5</a>]. These traits make CAM an attractive model for engineering improved water use efficiency and drought tolerance into crop plants that may be grown on more marginal land prone to seasonal droughts[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref006" class="ref-tip">6</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The CAM pathway is highly plastic and occurs along a continuum ranging from plants that are predominately C3 with weak or inducible CAM activity to constitutive species with highly optimized and efficient CAM. This diversity reflects the multiple origins of CAM and its utility in different environments [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref007" class="ref-tip">7</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref008" class="ref-tip">8</a>]. The biochemistry and physiology of CAM was worked out in species from the Crassulaceae, Cactaceae, and Bromeliaceae that exhibited strong constitutive CAM regardless of environmental conditions [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref009" class="ref-tip">9</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref010" class="ref-tip">10</a>]. Constitutive CAM plants often inhabit arid and semi-arid environments or live in epiphytic habitats subject to seasonal drought. In regions with seasonal or periodic drought, facultative and CAM cycling CAM species often display a flexible system of switching from C3 to CAM under drought conditions. Facultative CAM species have high nocturnal stomatal conductance under drought and CAM-cycling plants display typical C3 diel stomatal conductance, but re-fix respiratory CO<sub>2</sub> at night. The tremendous variation of CAM is partially explained by convergent evolution, since CAM has evolved independently at least 40 times across 35 diverse plant families [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref007" class="ref-tip">7</a>]. Model CAM species have emerged for several families and a recent wealth of genetic and genomic resources have advanced our understanding of CAM pathway evolution. The genomes of three constitutive CAM species have been sequenced including pineapple (<em>Ananas comosus</em>) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref011" class="ref-tip">11</a>], the orchid <em>Phalaenopsis equestris</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref012" class="ref-tip">12</a>], and the eudicot <em>Kalanchoe fedtschenkoi</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>]. Draft genomes of the facultative CAM orchids <em>Dendrobium catenatum</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref014" class="ref-tip">14</a>] and <em>D</em>. <em>officinale</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref015" class="ref-tip">15</a>] are also available, as are transcript and expression studies across the Agavoideae [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref016" class="ref-tip">16</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Facultative CAM and CAM-cycling species provide an excellent system for dissecting the molecular basis of CAM as we can directly compare expression dynamics between C3 and CAM gene networks. Comparisons between C3 and CAM states can be used to identify metabolite, physiology, and gene expression changes associated with CAM evolution. Transcriptome and metabolite surveys of the facultative CAM species <em>Talinum triangulare</em> identified a set of core CAM pathway genes and candidate transcription factors mediating photosynthetic plasticity [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref017" class="ref-tip">17</a>]. Though this study captured changes throughout drought progression, limited temporal resolution did not capture the circadian components of facultative CAM. Time series microarray data from ice plant (<em>Mesembryanthemum crystallinum</em>) also identified components of CAM induction, and suggested that there is a circadian regulated 4–8 hour phase shift underlying the C3 to CAM shift [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref018" class="ref-tip">18</a>]. Taking an evolutionary approach comparing cycling genes in C3, C4 and CAM (Agave) plants, it was found that CAM photosynthesis resulted from both accelerated evolution of specific protein domains as well as reprogramming of diel networks [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref019" class="ref-tip">19</a>]. However, it is still unclear how these time of day networks interact with the circadian clock and how the C3 to CAM switch is mediated within a single system.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>The circadian clock has evolved to optimize the daily timing, or phase, of cellular biology with the local external light and temperature cycles[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref020" class="ref-tip">20</a>]. In the model C3 species <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, between 30–50% of genes cycle under diel conditions with control by at least three evolutionarily conserved transcriptional modules defined by the morning (ME: CCACAC; Gbox: CACGTG), evening (EE: AATATCT; GATA: GGATA), and midnight (TBX: AAACCCT; SBX: AAGCCC; PBX: ATGGGCC) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>]. Moreover, this time of day transcriptional network ensures development and environment-specific growth through opposing phytohormone influences[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref022" class="ref-tip">22</a>], and is conserved across distantly related C3 and C4 species [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref023" class="ref-tip">23</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref024" class="ref-tip">24</a>]. The circadian clock directly controls components of the CAM pathway and is thought to coordinate the temporal oscillations of CAM. PPC is activated nocturnally via phosphorylation by the circadian clock activated phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase kinase (PPCK) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref025" class="ref-tip">25</a>]. Silencing of PPCK in <em>Kalanchoë fedtschenkoi</em> not only reduces CAM activity by ~66%, but also perturbs central components of the circadian clock, suggesting PPCK may be essential for clock robustness [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref026" class="ref-tip">26</a>]. Well-characterized CAM pathway genes in pineapple have acquired novel clock associated <em>cis</em>-elements compared to their orthologs from C3 and C4 species, suggesting a broad control of CAM by the core circadian clock [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref028" class="ref-tip">28</a>]. Expression patterns of clock genes are conserved under C3 and CAM mode in ice plant [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref029" class="ref-tip">29</a>] but the role of the clock in facultative and weak CAM induction remains unclear.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p5" name="article1.body1.sec1.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Here we generated a draft genome for the CAM-cycling species <em>Sedum album</em> using long read Single Molecule Real-Time (SMRT) sequencing and surveyed high-resolution temporal gene expression, metabolite, and physiology changes across a diel time course in well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM-cycling) conditions. This comparative approach revealed a massive time of day specific rewiring of the transcriptional networks in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> where only 20% of cycling genes overlap between the two conditions. This work demonstrates for the first time that a phase shift in the core circadian clock underpins a massive time of day rewiring that enables <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> to switch to CAM photosynthesis and overcome water stress.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec002" name="sec002" data-toc="sec002" class="link-target" title="Results"></a><h2>Results</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec003" name="sec003" class="link-target" title="Assembly and annotation of the complex Sedum album genome"></a>
<h3>Assembly and annotation of the complex Sedum album genome</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>We assembled a draft genome of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> to serve as a foundation resource to understand the genome-wide changes associated with the transition from C3 to drought induced CAM photosynthesis. We estimated the genome size to be 611 Mb based on flow cytometry, which was significantly larger than a previously reported value for <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> (142 Mb)[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref030" class="ref-tip">30</a>]. Karyotype analysis suggests <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> is tetraploid (2n = 4x = 68) with a deduced diploidized genome size of ~305 Mb (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s001">S1A Fig</a>). This is consistent with the kmer based genome size estimate, which revealed a heterozygous peak (first) with the full genome at 799 Mb and monoploid (second peak) at 256 Mb (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s001">S1B Fig</a>). Because of this complexity, we utilized a PacBio based SMRT sequencing approach to build the draft genome. We generated 4.4 million PacBio reads collectively spanning 33.7 Gb or 55x genome coverage. Raw PacBio reads were error corrected and assembled using the two leading PacBio assemblers: Falcon [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref031" class="ref-tip">31</a>] and Canu [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref032" class="ref-tip">32</a>]. Canu was able to accurately phase the highly similar homeologous regions and produced an assembly of 627 Mb across 15,256 contigs with an N50 of 47kb, designated as the S. album V2 genome. The average nucleotide similarity of homeologous regions is 99.6% based on the Canu assembly, suggesting <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> is either autotetraploid or allotetraploid with two highly similar genomes. Falcon collapsed the homeologous regions into a single haplotype with a total assembly size of 302 Mb across 6,038 contigs and an N50 of 93kb. The Canu and Falcon based contigs were polished to remove residual errors using high-coverage Illumina data with Pilon [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref033" class="ref-tip">33</a>].</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> assemblies were annotated using the MAKER-P pipeline [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref034" class="ref-tip">34</a>]. We utilized transcripts assembled from the timecourse RNAseq data (described below), and protein datasets from other angiosperms as evidence for <em>ab initio</em> gene prediction. After filtering transposon derived sequences, the tetraploid and diploidized assemblies contained 93,910 and 44,487 gene models respectively. The two resulting polished assemblies were highly complete with a BUSCO score of 97%. The tetraploid assembly is highly fragmented with lower contiguity, and partial gene models may explain the greater than 2x number of genes compared to the diploid assembly. We identified homeologous genes between the diploid and tetraploid assembly and compared their relative expression. Homeologs within the tetraploid assembly have low sequence divergence and most RNAseq reads map ambiguously to both homeologs, leading to similar transcript quantification for each homeolog in the pair. Comparison of expression between homologous genes in the tetraploid and diploid assemblies revealed a strong correlation across all timepoints collected from well-watered (C3) or drought (CAM) timecourses (C3 r = 0.927, CAM r = 0.901; <a href="#pgen.1008209.s002">S2 Fig</a>). Given the similarity in expression and for simplicity of downstream analyses, we proceeded with the diploidized assembly produced from Falcon, and this version is referred to as the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> V3 genome.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>We surveyed synteny and gene level collinearity between <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and the closely related constitutive CAM plant <em>Kalenchoe fedtschenkoi</em>. Synentic regions between the two genomes were largely collinear, with conserved gene content and order (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g001">Fig 1</a>). Roughly 71% of the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> V3 genome had detectable synteny with <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>, but this is likely an underestimation given the fragmented nature of both assemblies. Syntenic depth for each <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> region ranged from 1–5 to each region of <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>, reflecting the two shared whole genome duplications events in the Crassulaceae [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>] and polyploidy in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s003">S3 Fig</a>). Collinear regions were larger in <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> compared to <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, highlighting the relatively compact nature of the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> genome (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g001">Fig 1</a>). To estimate the divergence time of the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> subgenomes, we calculated Ks (synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) between homoeologous gene pairs based on synteny with <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>. We identified a single peak with a median Ks of 0.026, corresponding to an estimated divergence time of ~853,000 years (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s004">S4 Fig</a>). This supports a relatively recent polyploid origin of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g001" name="pgen-1008209-g001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">

              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">

                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 1. </span> Comparative genomics between the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> genomes.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Microsynteny between syntenic blocks of the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> (top) and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> (bottom) genomes. Syntenic gene pairs are shown by gray connections and genes are colored by orientation with blue and orange representing forward and reverse orientation respectively. The plotted region in Sedum spans 0.06–0.17 Mb on Contig_205 and 2.42–2.83 Mb on Scaffold55 in <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g001</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>We surveyed patterns of gene family dynamics across the genomes of seven CAM, five C3 and two C4 plants to identify expanded and contracted gene families in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. After normalizing for ploidy, we identified 33 gene families that were uniquely contracted in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and 41 that are uniquely expanded compared to the other species (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s012">S1 Table</a>). Among the contracted gene families in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> are ABA responsive proteins, sucrose transporter 2, Aluminum activated malate transporter 1, and several flavonoid biosynthesis pathways. Among the expanded gene families in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> are various transporters, photosynthesis related proteins, and early light induced proteins, among others (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s012">S1 Table</a>).</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec004" name="sec004" class="link-target" title="Global transcriptomic co-expression"></a>
<h3>Global transcriptomic co-expression</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>When <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> is subjected to drought conditions, it switches from robust uptake of CO<sub>2</sub> during the light phase to low rates of nocturnal carbon assimilation [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref035" class="ref-tip">35</a>], which is consistent with weak CAM-cycling induction (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g002">Fig 2</a>). We collected parallel diel timecourse data under well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM-cycling) conditions to identify genes, <em>cis-</em>elements and networks associated with the switch from C3 to CAM-cycling. The CAM-cycling timecourse was collected after 14 days of sustained drought when the CAM pathway was induced to conserve water usage (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g002">Fig 2A</a>). The well-watered (C3) <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> had mild diel titratable acid and stomatal aperture changes, whereas nocturnal acid accumulation and reduced stomatal aperture were observed under drought (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g002">Fig 2B and 2C</a>). Sedum has relatively low rates of carbon assimilation under drought conditions [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref036" class="ref-tip">36</a>], so it is difficult to speculate on stomatal conductance based on aperture alone. However, the stomatal aperture was reduced under drought compared to well-watered samples and with no significant changes in nocturnal stomatal aperture. Together, this suggests that <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> is a drought-inducible CAM-cycling plant, and much of the nocturnal carbon assimilation is likely from recycled respiratory CO<sub>2</sub>, as previously reported [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref037" class="ref-tip">37</a>].</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g002" name="pgen-1008209-g002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">

                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 2. </span> Overview of drought induced CAM-cycling in <em>Sedum album</em>.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(A) Well-watered <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> plants performing C3 (top) and plants performing CAM-cycling after 14 days of drought (bottom). (B) Titratable acid of well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM) treated <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> across the surveyed timecourse. Three replicates were collected per timepoint. Standard error bars are shown. (C) Stomatal aperture in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. Stomatal width and length were measured diurnally (ZT06) and nocturnally (ZT22) in C3 and CAM-cycling conditions and divided to calculate aperture (n&gt;10). Standard deviation bars are shown.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g002</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Parallel sampling of C3 (well-watered) and CAM-cycling (14-day drought) plants were collected every two hours over a 24-hour diel timecourse in triplicate for RNA sequencing and metabolite measurements. Differentially expressed genes between pairwise C3 and CAM-cycling timepoints varied from 702 to 3,245 genes, reflecting massive transcriptional reprogramming (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s012">S1 Table</a>). GO enrichment analysis of these differential expressed genes indicated that most of the up-regulated genes under drought and CAM induction were related to abscisic acid responses, oxidative stress, and water deprivation at both day and night. Genes responding to ethylene were mainly enriched at night (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s013">S2 Table</a>). Down-regulated differential expressed genes in the CAM samples were enriched with genes involved in cell wall metabolism, organization and biogenesis reflecting the reduced growth under drought conditions. There was also an over-representation of down-regulated genes related to photosynthesis at night (10pm-4am) in drought stressed <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>After filtering genes with low expression (total TPM &lt; 5), 29,372 genes were used to construct a differential co-expression network utilizing a weighted correlation network analysis (WGCNA) approach [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref038" class="ref-tip">38</a>]. This approach grouped genes into 32 and 25 co-expression modules for the C3 and CAM-cycling networks respectively. Preservation of modules between the C3 and CAM networks was low (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g003">Fig 3A</a>), consistent with massive rewiring of the expression network under drought stress and CAM induction. The core CAM pathway genes (described in more detail below) were found in different modules in the CAM-cycling network, suggesting regulation of CAM and C3 pathways through distinct transcriptional programs and network rewiring.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g003" name="pgen-1008209-g003"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 3. </span> Gene co-expression and interaction networks between the well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM-cycling) <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.fig2.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.fig2.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(A) A consensus matrix including the 32 modules for the C3 network and 25 modules for the CAM-cycling network is shown. The number of genes shared between any two modules is shown in the matrix and numbers highlighted in red have significant overlap (Fisher’s exact test), with the percentage of shared genes depicted by a red gradient. Gene interaction networks of C3 (B) and CAM-cycling (C) timecourses are plotted and core CAM pathway genes and their interacting partners are highlighted in yellow.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g003</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Photosynthesis and stress related pathways were co-regulated in the drought induced CAM-cycling co-expression network. The C3 network module 3 and CAM-cycling network module 7 were enriched in photosynthesis-related GO terms such as photosynthetic electron transport chain (GO:0022900), response to light stimulus (GO:009416), and photosynthesis light harvesting in photosystem I (GO:0009765; <a href="#pgen.1008209.s014">S3</a> and <a href="#pgen.1008209.s015">S4</a> Tables). CAM-cycling network module 7 was also enriched with GO terms related to stress response (GO:0006950) and oxidative stress (GO:0006979) compared to the orthologous module in the C3 network, which had no enrichment. GO terms related to photosystem II assembly and function were over-represented in the C3 network module 3 but not in the corresponding CAM-cycling module 7. This indicates photosystem I and electron transport chain pathways are co-regulated with stress related genes under drought but not photosystem II. This is likely due to drought induced damage on photosystem II and the tightly coordinated regulation of stress responsive and photosynthesis pathways to mitigate photooxidative and drought associated damage. This hypothesis is further supported by the enriched GO terms in CAM-cycling network module 15 and 19, which contain ATP metabolic process, ATP hydrolysis coupled protein transport, and superoxide metabolic processes (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s014">S3</a> and <a href="#pgen.1008209.s015">S4</a> Tables). These GO terms were not enriched in any well-watered, C3 network modules. Reactive oxygen species scavengers accumulate during water deficit to prevent oxidative damage [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref039" class="ref-tip">39</a>], and scavengers such as superoxide dismutase are up-regulated in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> under drought stress (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s005">S5 Fig</a>). The comparison of gene co-expression networks between well-watered (C3) and drought conditions (CAM-cycling) support the tight regulation between photosynthesis and stress responses.</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec005" name="sec005" class="link-target" title="Drought and CAM associated diel regulation and phase changes"></a>
<h3>Drought and CAM associated diel regulation and phase changes</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In pineapple, core CAM pathway genes have diel expression patterns along with enriched circadian associated <em>cis-</em>elements [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref028" class="ref-tip">28</a>]. Most drought related genes are circadian regulated [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref040" class="ref-tip">40</a>] and proper diurnal expression is associated with growth and resilience under water deficit [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref041" class="ref-tip">41</a>–<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref043" class="ref-tip">43</a>]. Therefore, we also estimated the periodicity, phase (peak time of expression) and amplitude differences of each <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> gene under either C3 or CAM-cycling using the JTK_CYCLE program [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref044" class="ref-tip">44</a>]. There were 10,278 (35%) and 11,946 (41%) genes cycling under C3 and CAM-cycling conditions respectively (<a href="#pgen-1008209-t001">Table 1</a>). These numbers are similar to those reported for Arabidopsis [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>] and other species [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref024" class="ref-tip">24</a>] under diel conditions of 12 light/12 hrs dark (LD) and thermocycles (HC: hot/cold). Surprisingly, only 22% (6,480) genes cycled under both conditions, and of these shared cycling genes, 39% (2,504) had a change in amplitude and 75% (4,579) had a change in phase. In addition, almost 50% of genes displayed a unique pattern of cycling in only one condition. These results corroborate the differential expression and network analysis, which is consistent with a massive time of day rewiring of the transcriptional machinery in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> under drought and during the switch to CAM-cycling photosynthesis.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-t001" name="pgen-1008209-t001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 1. </span> Summary of gene circadian rhythm changes between well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM-cycling) timecourses.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t001</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>To understand the shifts in the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> circadian clock during C3-CAM conversion, we looked at cycling expression patterns (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s016">S5 Table</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.s006">S6 Fig</a>). First, we found an expansion of core clock genes in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> compared to Arabidopsis, with large changes in circadian clock associated 1/late elongated hypocotyl (<em>CCA1/LHY</em>, 7 vs. 2), PRRs (10 vs. 4), early flowering 3 (<em>ELF3</em>, 5 vs. 2) and lux arrhythmo (<em>LUX</em>, 7 vs. 5) and additional copies of gigantea (<em>GI</em>) and cryptochrome 1 (<em>CRY1</em>) (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s017">S6</a> and <a href="#pgen.1008209.s018">S7</a> Tables). Many of these expansions were shared with <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>], pineapple[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>], and orchid[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref012" class="ref-tip">12</a>], suggesting gene expansion may be linked to the circadian regulation of CAM pathways (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s018">S7 Table</a>). While none of the CAM species have a copy of the AtPRR9 ortholog, there were two PRRs only found in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s007">S7 Fig</a>). In general, the phase of the cycling of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> circadian clock gene orthologs matched those of Arabidopsis [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref024" class="ref-tip">24</a>] but they showed a phase shift under drought stress (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s017">S6 Table</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.s006">S6 Fig</a>). While <em>GI</em>, <em>CCA1/LHY</em> and <em>ELF3</em> maintained the phase of expression between C3 and CAM in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, the amplitude of <em>GI</em> and <em>ELF3</em> decreased under CAM (25–50%) and <em>CCA1/LHY</em> amplitude increased (25%) under CAM. In contrast, most of the <em>PRR1</em>s, <em>PRR5</em>s (but not <em>PRR7/3</em>s) displayed a 1–4 hr phase shift (later) under CAM-cycling conditions, which is consistent with the global 1–4 hr phase shift (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s016">S5 Table</a>). By comparing the phase and amplitude changes observed in drought treated <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> with the drought treated C3 plant <em>Brassica rapa</em> and two CAM systems (<em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em> and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>) (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s017">S6 Table</a>), we were able to untangle the circadian rhythmicity change unique to drought treated <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. Shared amplitude changes between <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em> may represent universal drought responses and those that are unique to <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> represent lineage specific drought responses and changes related to CAM induction. We identified several core clock genes with divergent changes in phase and amplitude. For instance, <em>CRY1</em> genes were cycling in both conditions of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> but only under well-watered conditions of <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em> (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s017">S6 Table</a>), suggesting the circadian rhythm of <em>CRY1</em> was conserved in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> under drought stress. In addition, flavin-binding, kelch repeat, f box (<em>FKF</em>) displayed cycling pattern in all three CAM plants surveyed but not in the two conditions of <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Interestingly, we observed a phase shift where genes with rhythmic expression in well-watered conditions were over-represented at phases 11–17 while genes with rhythmic expression under drought were enriched in later phases (phases 18–23) (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g004">Fig 4A</a>). This observed phase changing is not solely caused by drought or CAM photosynthesis but an interaction of both, as drought treated <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em> and CAM performing <em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em> have different responses (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g004">Fig 4A</a>). Therefore, the phase shift of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> genes under drought is possibly caused by the involvement of both circadian and drought-related <em>cis</em>-element, and expression shift of the circadian clock genes mentioned above. We identified a complete conservation of the time of day overrepresentation of the diel and circadian-related <em>cis</em>-elements, consistent with what we have found in other plant systems[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref023" class="ref-tip">23</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref024" class="ref-tip">24</a>]. In addition, the time of day overrepresentation of both the evening specific <em>cis</em>-elements, evening elements and TBX, displayed a 1–4 hr phase shift between C3 and CAM-cycling, while the morning specific elements ME and Gbox did not (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s008">S8A Fig</a>). Furthermore, there were many more significant evening-specific <em>cis</em>-elements under CAM that were distinct from conserved elements (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s008">S8B Fig</a>), including 8 novel drought/CAM-specific <em>cis</em>-elements in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> that are enriched in specific phases of drought but not well-watered (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g004">Fig 4B</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.s019">S8 Table</a>). These elements were mainly enriched at the later phase, which is consistent with the phase shift of drought-specific cycling genes we observed. While all eight elements were over-represented in drought-specific cycling genes (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s020">S9 Table</a>), elements CAM-1 and CAM-7 were also enriched in cycling genes that had higher amplitude in drought condition (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g004">Fig 4C</a>). These eight novel <em>cis</em>-elements support our findings that the time of day networks are completely rewired under drought induced CAM photosynthesis, and that is not just a phasing of the C3 networks but instead a new drought and CAM specific network.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g004" name="pgen-1008209-g004"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 4. </span> CAM induced phase shift and identification of novel drought cycling <em>cis</em>-elements.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(A) Phase-specific enrichment of cycling genes in well-watered or drought conditions in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em>, and cycling genes in photosynthetic and non-photosynthetic leaf tissue of <em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em>. (B) Enrichment of three novel drought/CAM-specific <em>cis</em>-elements in each phase under well-watered and drought timecoruses of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. (C) Enrichment analysis of novel elements in each gene category. A significance line (<em>p</em>-value of 0.05) is plotted as a dashed line.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g004</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>In addition to the novel <em>cis</em>-elements, genes with CAM and drought specific cycling or higher amplitude in CAM were enriched with the stress-mediated ABRE (YACGTGGC) and ABRE-like (BACGTGKM) <em>cis</em>-element in their 2kb upstream promoter sequences (<a href="#pgen-1008209-t001">Table 1</a>). CAM specific rhythmic genes were also enriched with the MYB transcription factor binding (MACCWAMC) <em>cis</em>-elements. In contrast, cycling genes with higher amplitude under C3 were enriched with the circadian related evening element [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref045" class="ref-tip">45</a>] and MYC2 transcription factor binding site. To understand the biological functions of genes in each category, we performed a GO enrichment test. Genes with cycling expression in C3 only were mainly enriched with GO terms related DNA replication, translation and protein related metabolic process. Enriched GO terms in genes with CAM specific cycling were mainly related to transcription and gene expression regulation by RNA. Genes that are cycling in both conditions, but with higher amplitude in CAM were over-represented by GO terms involved in stress response including water deprivation (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s021">S10 Table</a>).</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec006" name="sec006" class="link-target" title="Identification of core CAM pathway genes through comparative genomics"></a>
<h3>Identification of core CAM pathway genes through comparative genomics</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Genes in the core CAM pathway belong to large gene families shared across plant genomes, and most have functions unrelated to CAM activity. Independent CAM lineages show evidence of convergent amino acid substitutions in core enzymes [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>], but independent recruitment of the same orthologs remains untested for most CAM genes. Through comparing diel expression patterns under C3 and CAM induction, we identified <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> genes associated with the core CAM pathway and putative vacuolar transporters involved in metabolite transport. We also compared the annotated <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> CAM genes with their pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> orthologs to identify similarities among the three sequenced CAM plants with diel expression data [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref028" class="ref-tip">28</a>]. Putative CAM pathway genes in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> had higher expression (<em>PPC</em>, <em>PPCK</em>, <em>NADP</em><sup>+</sup><em>-ME</em>, <em>MDH</em>, <em>PPDK</em>) or become oscillating (<em>PPCK</em>, <em>NAD-ME</em>) under drought induced CAM-cycling compared to C3 (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g005">Fig 5</a>, <a href="#pgen-1008209-t002">Table 2</a>). Core CAM pathway genes were identified in the constitutive CAM plants <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> and pineapple using a similar approach [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>].</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g005" name="pgen-1008209-g005"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g005"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g005" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g005"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g005" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g005">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g005</a></p></div><a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-t002" name="pgen-1008209-t002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 2. </span> Circadian pattern and amplitude changes of CAM photosynthesis genes between well-watered and drought <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t002</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Beta carbonic anhydrase (<em>β-CA</em>) converts CO<sub>2</sub> to HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>-</sup> in the first step of nocturnal carboxylation in CAM photosynthesis. None of the annotated <em>β-CA</em> genes in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> had high nocturnal expression under drought induced CAM-cycling compared to C3 (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s006">S6 Fig</a>). Low <em>β-CA</em> expression was also observed in the drought inducible CAM plant <em>Talinum triangulare</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref017" class="ref-tip">17</a>] and constitutive CAM species <em>Yucca aloifolia</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref046" class="ref-tip">46</a>]. In contrast, <em>β-CA</em> from pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> had strong nocturnal expression, which may support a higher conversion rate of CO<sub>2</sub> to HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>-</sup> (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s009">S9 Fig</a>). Nocturnal carbon assimilation in the weak CAM plant <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> likely occurs at a much lower rate than constitutive CAM species. Thus, <em>β-CA</em> may not be essential to provide additional bicarbonate for phospho<em>enol</em>pyruvate carboxylation in the dark period during CAM-cycling in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Phospho<em>enol</em>pyruvate carboxylase (<em>PPC</em>) and Phospho<em>enol</em>pyruvate carboxylase kinase (<em>PPCK</em>) are the core CAM genes that mediate the fixation of CO<sub>2</sub> at night. Under CAM induction, these genes had up to 73x fold higher expression than C3. <em>PPC</em> (Sal_001109) has high expression during the day while <em>PPCK</em> genes (Sal_021277, Sal_045582, Sal_050851) were expressed mainly nocturnally (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g005">Fig 5</a>). <em>PPC</em> transcript expression was slightly shifted compared to pineapple (Aco010025.1) and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> (Kaladp0095s0055.1), while <em>PPCK</em> expression pattern was more conserved across the three CAM species (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g006">Fig 6A</a>). Although there are multiple copies of <em>PPC</em> in each plant genome, <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> recruited the same <em>PPC</em> ortholog for CAM (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g006">Fig 6B</a>). Given the unique monocot and eudicot specific duplications of PPC, it is not possible to assess if the same PPC was recruited in monocot and eudicot CAM plants. Based on the conserved diurnal expression of <em>PPC</em>, we surveyed <em>cis</em>-element enrichment across CAM, C4, and C3 orthologs to identify <em>cis-</em>elements related to circadian regulation. The morning element [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>] circadian clock motif was present in the <em>PPC</em> promoters from all three CAM plants, as well as the two C4 plants (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g006">Fig 6B</a>). In addition, TCP and MYB transcription factor binding sites were also present in the three CAM <em>PPC</em> promoters.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g006" name="pgen-1008209-g006"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 6. </span> Conserved transcript expression of core CAM genes and recruitment of <em>PPC</em> during CAM evolution.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.fig2.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.fig2.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(A-C) Transcript expression of <em>PPC</em>, <em>PPCK</em> and <em>MDH</em> genes in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, <em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em> and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> are plotted. (D) Maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree of <em>PPC</em> orthologs in 10 species. The bootstrap support values are indicated on each node. The <em>PPC</em> copies used by CAM and C4 plants are highlighted in blue and orange, respectively. Clock associated <em>cis</em>-elements located in the 2kb upstream promoter of PPC are denoted in blue (morning element, ME), yellow (TCP transcription factor), and green (MYB transcription factor).</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g006</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Based on expression dynamics between conditions, diurnal decarboxylation in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> is likely driven by chloroplastic NADP<sup>+</sup>-malic enzyme (<em>NADP</em><sup><em>+</em></sup><em>-ME</em>, Sal_015733) and pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (<em>PPDK</em>, Sal_044029; <a href="#pgen-1008209-g005">Fig 5</a>). <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> also utilizes the ME and PPDK pathway but pineapple primarily utilizes the phospho<em>enol</em>pyruvate carboxykinase pathway (Aco010232.1) for decarboxylation [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref013" class="ref-tip">13</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>]. Nocturnally fixed CO<sub>2</sub> is converted to malate and stored in the vacuole to avoid cytosolic pH fluctuations and prevent feedback inhibition of PPC [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref010" class="ref-tip">10</a>]. While the proton pumps required to establish a gradient in the vacuole are well-characterized [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref047" class="ref-tip">47</a>], the malate transporters remain uncharacterized in CAM plants. The most likely transporters responsible for malate influx and efflux are homologs of the Arabidopsis clade II aluminum activated malate transporter (<em>ALMT</em> 3/4/5/6/9) and tonoplast dicarboxylate transporter respectively [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref028" class="ref-tip">28</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref048" class="ref-tip">48</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref049" class="ref-tip">49</a>]. However, under CAM-cycling conditions, no <em>S</em>. <em>album ALMT</em> genes shared a conserved transcript pattern with pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>. The tonoplast dicarboxylate transporter ortholog in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> had peak expression in the early morning in both C3 and CAM-cycling mode, but the expression drops to almost zero at dusk (ZT12) in CAM-cycling. The sudden expression decrease coincided with the titratable acid accumulation (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g001">Fig 1B</a>). This expression pattern was similar in pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> where their expression decreases sharply (&gt;10 fold reduction) before sunset and gradually increases before sunrise (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s010">S10 Fig</a>). This supports a potential conserved role of tonoplast dicarboxylate transporter as a malate exporter across CAM plants.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec4.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>To elucidate the potential regulators of CAM-cycling photosynthesis, gene regulatory networks were constructed and compared between the C3 and CAM timecourses and putative activators and suppressors controlling core CAM genes were identified (<a href="#pgen-1008209-t003">Table 3</a>; <a href="#pgen-1008209-g003">Fig 3B and 3C</a>). Core CAM pathway genes shared few interactors between C3 and CAM-cycling supporting independent networks. <em>PPC</em> expression in the CAM-cycling network was associated with the appearance of 12 putative activators and the disappearance of 2 suppressors. The three copies of phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase kinase (<em>PPCK</em>) showed similar regulatory patterns. Interestingly, one of the <em>PPCK</em> copies (Sal_021277-RA) interacted with the ABA biosynthesis gene 9-cis-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenase (Sal_006713-RA), an ABA exporter (Sal_009291-RA) and an ABA receptor regulator (Sal_007192-RA) under C3 conditions and this interaction disappeared when <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> switched to CAM-cycling under drought. <em>PPCK</em> phosphorylates PPC at night to reduce the allosteric inhibition of PPC by malate. This phosphorylation process is under circadian control [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref026" class="ref-tip">26</a>] and the changes of interactions of core clock genes with <em>PPCK</em> are evident in our gene interaction networks. Two <em>CCA1/LHY</em> gene copies (Sal_047094-RA and Sal_051928-RA) interacted with <em>PPCK</em> (Sal_021277-RA) under both conditions but two additional <em>CCA1/LHY</em> copies (Sal_050684-RA and Sal_051929) only interacted with <em>PPCK</em> under well-watered condition while <em>CCA1/LHY_C1</em> (Sal_047093-RA) and <em>PPCK</em> only interacted in drought treated <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. The only other CAM core gene that had putative gene interactions with core clock gene <em>CCA1/LHY</em> was malic enzyme (<em>ME</em>), where some of these interactions were only observed in the drought network (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s022">S11 Table</a>). Malate dehydrogenase (<em>MDH</em>) was potentially controlled by 312 activators and 2 suppressors, including phosphoglycerate mutase (Sal_015714-RA), which is involved in glycolysis and gluconeogenesis. Four additional glycolysis and gluconeogenesis pathway genes (Sal_003892-RA, Sal_043275-RA, Sal_048516-RA, and Sal_050408-RA) were also predicted to be involved in activating <em>ME</em> expression in CAM photosynthesis. In addition, starch synthesis pathway genes including ADP glucose pyrophosphorylase large subunit (Sal_005475-RA and Sal_005955-RA) and starch branching enzyme (Sal_041721-RA) were potential activators for <em>ME</em> and pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (<em>PPDK</em>) respectively. Our gene regulatory networks demonstrate a direct link between CAM photosynthesis and carbohydrate metabolism (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g003">Fig 3B</a>, c) and the drought induction of CAM photosynthesis requires the rewiring of large number of existing genes, similar to patterns observed in pineapple [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref027" class="ref-tip">27</a>].</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-t003" name="pgen-1008209-t003"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t003"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t003" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t003"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t003" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
