              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.t003</a></p></div></div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec007" name="sec007" class="link-target" title="Regulation of transitory starch metabolism during the C3-CAM conversion"></a>
<h3>Regulation of transitory starch metabolism during the C3-CAM conversion</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Nocturnal synthesis of PEP is essential for maintaining high levels of CAM activity. PEP is supplied through the degradation of transitory starch or soluble sugars [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref050" class="ref-tip">50</a>], and mutants deficient in starch degradation have impaired CAM activity [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref051" class="ref-tip">51</a>]. Transitory starch accumulated in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> throughout the day and was depleted nocturnally in both C3 and CAM, but the total amount of starch synthesized was higher when <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> was operating in CAM-cycling mode (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g007">Fig 7</a>). A large proportion of genes in the starch biosynthesis pathway (phosphoglucoseisomerase, starch branching enzymes, granular bound starch synthase and glucan water dikinase) had higher expression in CAM-cycling than C3 (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s011">S11A Fig</a>). Starch is mainly synthesized in the stroma from glucose 6-phosphate (G6P) supplied from the Calvin Benson cycle through the conversion of F6P to G6P via glucose-6-phosphate isomerase (<em>PGI</em>). G6P can also be imported from the cytosol through a glucose 6-phosphate/phosphate translocator (<em>GPT</em>). The chloroplastic PGI is tightly regulated by multiple factors in Arabidopsis and this can limit the flux of starch synthesis [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref052" class="ref-tip">52</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref053" class="ref-tip">53</a>]. When the required flux of starch synthesis exceeds the limit set by PGI (such as the switch to CAM), plants may bypass the rate limiting plastidic <em>PGI</em> pathway by exporting carbon as triose phosphates. Carbon from cytosolic <em>PGI</em> can then be remobilized to the plastid as hexose phosphate using the <em>GPT</em>. Genes involved in this <em>PGI</em> bypass pathway were upregulated in CAM compared to C3 including the triose phosphate/phosphate translocator (Sal_024485-RA) (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s011">S11B Fig</a>). Most of the transcripts for genes involved in the branch of the pentose phosphate pathway from glucose 6-phosphate to ribulose 5-phosphate (G6P shunt) in both the plastid and cytosol were down-regulated in the CAM mode (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s011">S11C Fig</a>). Together, these data suggest that metabolism in CAM-cycling <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> is shifted to increase starch synthesis while avoiding carbon loss through the G6P shunt, which is consistent with the hypothesis previously proposed by Sharkey and Weise [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref054" class="ref-tip">54</a>].</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g007" name="pgen-1008209-g007"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 7. </span> Transitory starch associated metabolites across the C3 and CAM-cycling timecourses.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.fig1.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.fig1.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Glucose, sucrose, fructose, fructose 6-phosphate, glucose 6-phosphate, β-maltose and starch were measured on the well-watered (C3, blue) and drought (CAM, red) <em>Sedum album</em> every 2 hours for 24 hours. An average of 3 replicates was plotted for each timepoint and the standard error is shaded.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g007</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>There are plastidic <em>GPTs</em> in plants, however they are not normally expressed in autotrophic tissue. It is hypothesized that this transporter is not expressed to prevent the pool of G6P from reaching the K<sub>m</sub> threshold of glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase in the chloroplast [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref054" class="ref-tip">54</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref055" class="ref-tip">55</a>]. A large pool of G6P in the chloroplast could result in a futile cycle and loss of carbon as CO<sub>2</sub> through the G6P shunt [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref054" class="ref-tip">54</a>]. A <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> ortholog to the Arabidopsis <em>GPT2</em> gene (Sal_018693-RA) had peak daytime expression in both C3 and CAM-cycling but showed drastically increased expression (ranged from 11 to 205-fold higher) in CAM-cycling condition (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g008">Fig 8</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.s023">S12 Table</a>). A similar diurnal expression pattern was observed in the constitutive CAM plants pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>, but not the C3 plant Arabidopsis (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g008">Fig 8B</a>). In contrast, the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> GPT1 ortholog had constitutive expression in both C3 and CAM-cycling, and is likely not associated with CAM activity (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s024">S13 Table</a>). Expression dynamics between CAM and C3 indicates that the starch synthesis from G6P in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> might be regulated at multiple pathways including starch synthesis, plastidic <em>PGI</em> bypass, G6P shunt, as well as triose phosphate and G6P transporters as summarized in <a href="#pgen-1008209-g008">Fig 8C</a>.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pgen-1008209-g008" name="pgen-1008209-g008"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Fig 8. </span> Conserved expression of glucose 6-phosphate transporter 2 (<em>GPT2</em>) across CAM plants and regulation of starch metabolism in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>.</div><p class="caption_target"><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.fig2.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.fig2.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>(A) Maximum likelihood phylogeny of <em>GPT1</em> and <em>GPT2</em> among 10 species. (B) Transcript expression of <em>GPT2</em> in C3 timecourses (Arabidopsis and well-watered <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>), CAM-cycling (drought induced <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>) and constitutive CAM (pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>) is plotted. (C) Schematic diagram representing the regulation of starch synthesis, cytosolic PGI bypass, cytosolic and plastidic G6P shunt in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> under CAM-cycling photosynthesis. Abbreviations are as follows: GAP: glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate; DHAP: dihydroxyacetone phosphate; F6P: fructose 6-phosphate; G6P: glucose 6-phosphate; FBP: fructose 1,6-bisphosphate; Ru5P: ribulose 5-phosphate; TPT: triose phosphate/phosphate translocator; GPT2: glucose 6-phosphate/phosphate translocator 2.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.g008</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec5.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>At night, transitory starch is broken down into either maltose and glucose via the hydrolytic degradation pathway, or to G6P via the phosphorolytic degradation pathway [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref056" class="ref-tip">56</a>]. It is thought that starch degradation shifts from hydrolytic to phosphorolytic pathway during the transition from C3 to CAM in ice plant [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref056" class="ref-tip">56</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref057" class="ref-tip">57</a>]. Chloroplasts isolated from the ice plant under C3 photosynthesis mainly export maltose during starch degradation and switch to G6P export under CAM. We measured the total G6P and maltose contents of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> across the C3 and CAM diurnal timecourses. C3 performing <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> had a higher abundance of maltose than CAM-cycling plants at night but the G6P content was similar in both C3 and CAM-cycling plants (<a href="#pgen-1008209-g007">Fig 7</a>). We also examined the transcript level of genes proposed to be involved in the two starch degradation pathways (hydrolytic and phosphorolytic) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref056" class="ref-tip">56</a>]. Homologs of Arabidopsis glucan phosphorylase (<em>LSF2</em>), β-amylase 6 and β-amylase 9 were expressed higher in well-watered conditions but glucan phosphorylase (<em>SEX4</em>), β-amylase 2, β-amylase 3, β-amylase 7 and isoamylase 3 were expressed strongly under drought (<a href="#pgen.1008209.s011">S11D Fig</a>). Hydrolytic pathway genes were not universally upregulated in either photosynthetic condition. This indicates that either <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> does not switch its starch degradation pathways during the C3-CAM conversion, or the regulation of this shift was not controlled at the transcriptional level.</p>
</div>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec008" name="sec008" data-toc="sec008" class="link-target" title="Discussion"></a><h2>Discussion</h2><a id="article1.body1.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Facultative CAM and CAM-cycling plants are optimized for rapid growth under favorable C3 conditions and sustained resilience under drought through CAM related water conservation. This reversible mechanism makes facultative CAM and CAM-cycling plants an ideal model to dissect the genetic basis of CAM and for engineering improved water use efficiency and drought tolerance in crop plants. Engineering inducible CAM photosynthesis into C3 crops could improve water use efficiency and resilience under prolonged drought stress while maintaining high yields when field conditions are ideal. Establishing high quality genomic resources are an essential foundation for downstream functional genomics of CAM. The complex polyploidy of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> presented a computational challenge, but through a combination of long read sequencing and optimized assembly we produced a high quality, diploidized reference.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>High-resolution diurnal expression data for both C3 (well-watered) and CAM-cycling (drought) provided a comparative system for identifying genes and pathways involved in CAM. Utilizing a comparative differential co-expression approach, we found a strong link between stress responses and photosynthesis, supporting the tight regulation of CAM photosynthesis by drought networks. The CAM-cycling and C3 co-expression networks have a low degree of overlap, supporting massive reprogramming of genes related to drought and CAM photosynthesis induction. This is further supported by the gene regulatory networks, which showed major shifts in gene interaction under CAM induction. In addition, the large-scale rewiring of gene rhythmicity and phase underlies the drought protective mechanism in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. The time of day reprogramming is comprised of phase and amplitude change of core clock genes, and expression regulation under known and novel drought/CAM-specific <em>cis-</em>elements, which in turn lead to a phase difference between well-watered and drought cycling genes. The genome level phase shift of transcript expression is uniquely observed in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and this pattern was not observed in drought treated <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em> and the constitutive CAM plant <em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em>. This highlights the complexity of CAM-cycling induction and the tight coordination of multiple metabolic pathways including stress responses, photosynthesis, and circadian rhythm.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p3" name="article1.body1.sec3.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>We observed conserved diurnal transcript expression of core CAM genes (<em>PPCK</em>, <em>ME</em>, <em>PPDK</em>) and potential transporters among independent CAM lineages, but patterns are not conserved for every gene (i.e. <em>PPC</em>, <em>MDH</em>). Though <em>PPC</em> is highly expressed in all CAM plants, the diel oscillation varies between species. PPC orthologs from the same clade (PPC-1 and not PPC-2) are always recruited for CAM related carboxylation [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref058" class="ref-tip">58</a>] and the copies from the CAM species surveyed have common <em>cis</em>-regulatory elements related to circadian regulation. Based on our transcript data, <em>β-CA</em> likely has minimal function in CAM-cycling <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> compared to the constitutive CAM pathway where strong nocturnal expression of <em>β-CA</em> might be essential to rapidly assimilate and store carbon. The non-cycling expression of <em>β-CA</em> was also reported in the inducible CAM plant <em>Talinum triangulare</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref017" class="ref-tip">17</a>]. We hypothesize that the nocturnal <em>β-CA</em> expression is coupled with the high nocturnal stomatal conductance in constitutive CAM plants, which enhances the nocturnal CO<sub>2</sub> fixation rate. <em>β-CA</em> involved in normal metabolic processes in the cytosol is probably sufficient for CAM activity in weak, low assimilating species, but enzymatic activity remains to be surveyed. <em>β-CA</em> is not essential for efficient operation of the C4 pathway in maize [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref059" class="ref-tip">59</a>], but the dispensability of <em>β-CA</em> in CAM is untested.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p4" name="article1.body1.sec3.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Transitory starch content is high in CAM plants to provide enough substrate to regenerate PEP. CAM induced expression changes suggest <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> increases the transitory starch content through multiple pathways. Starch biosynthesis pathway genes have increased expression under CAM-cycling compared to C3. During CAM-cycling, G6P transporters and triose phosphate transporters are upregulated and genes involved in the G6P shunt are downregulated. The diurnal induction of <em>GPT</em> transcripts and transport activity under CAM has been reported in ice plant previously [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref060" class="ref-tip">60</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref061" class="ref-tip">61</a>]. Our high-resolution transcriptome data showed that expression of <em>GPT2</em> increased after CAM-cycling induction while <em>GPT1</em> had similar expression in both conditions. Therefore, <em>GPT2</em> is the likely controller of G6P translocation in CAM-cycling photosynthesis. In C3 plants, the G6P pool in the stroma is 3 to 20 times lower than the cytosol [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref062" class="ref-tip">62</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref063" class="ref-tip">63</a>]. We thus speculate that CAM plants activate <em>GPT2</em> in the daytime to import G6P into the stroma for transitory starch synthesis, this would bypass the plastidic PGI which can be kinetically limiting to starch synthesis and result in higher stromal G6P concentration. Interestingly, the <em>S</em>. <em>album GPT2</em> had a low but consistent diurnal oscillation under C3 conditions while the Arabidopsis <em>GPT2</em> has low constitutive expression. Inducible CAM plants might have C3-CAM intermediate transcript expression for certain genes in order to rapidly switch between photosynthetic modes in response to abiotic stress.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p5" name="article1.body1.sec3.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Concentrations of total cellular G6P and maltose and transcript expression of the starch degradation pathway genes revealed the complexity of PEP regeneration during the C3-CAM transition. The minimal change of G6P content between well-watered and drought conditions could be due to high cytosolic G6P content masking the G6P produced through phosphorolytic starch degradation. It could also indicate that there is no increase in phosphorolytic starch degradation during C3-CAM conversion. The increase in transcript expression of many hydrolytic pathway genes might suggest that there is no switch of starch degradation pathways from hydrolytic to phosphorolytic in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, contrasting pattern observed in ice plant [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref060" class="ref-tip">60</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref061" class="ref-tip">61</a>]. However, the starch degradation patterns observed in ice plant were assayed with isolated chloroplasts, and <em>in vivo</em> degradation patterns may differ when concentrations of G6P and maltose in both the chloroplast and cytosol become kinetically relevant. Alternatively, the shift of starch degradation pathways could be species specific.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p6" name="article1.body1.sec3.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>In <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, C3 to CAM-cycling switching results from a complete rewiring of the time of day networks. We found that there was a specific drought/CAM network that leverages a novel set of <em>cis</em>-elements. While the circadian <em>cis</em>-elements are completely conserved under C3 conditions, an alternative transcriptional module is leveraged to engage CAM photosynthesis. Underlying this alternative pathway may be a new repertoire of core circadian genes since we observed a significant increase in these genes as well as distinct phasing and amplitude under CAM-cycling conditions. Core circadian genes have been shown to be retained after whole genome duplication (WGD) and these new gene copies may provide a means to augmented stress responses.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p7" name="article1.body1.sec3.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>Drought responsive and CAM pathways are intrinsically linked in facultative and CAM-cycling plants. Disentangling these two processes would ideally require a C3 <em>Sedum</em> outgroup with no detectable CAM-cycling activity. CAM occurs along a continuum within <em>Sedum</em>, ranging from CAM-cycling and facultative to constitutive[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref036" class="ref-tip">36</a>]. <em>Sedum</em> is highly polyphyletic[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref064" class="ref-tip">64</a>] and the C3 Eurasian species are in phylogenetically distinct clades, making detailed comparisons with CAM lineages challenging. Furthermore, CAM anatomy and gene expression predate CAM origins within <em>Yucca</em> and more broadly across Agavoideae [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref046" class="ref-tip">46</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref065" class="ref-tip">65</a>], and CAM-like signatures of gene expression under drought may be observed in C3 <em>Sedum</em> species. The phase shift during CAM-cycling induction in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> was not observed in similar drought timecourses from <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em> [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref043" class="ref-tip">43</a>], and few genes with cycling expression are overlapping. This suggests the massive shifts of expression in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> are driven by CAM related activities and not simply conserved drought responses. More detailed comparisons are needed to parse pathways related to CAM and drought in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>. Genomic and high-resolution transcriptomic data from <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> provide a valuable foundation for downstream functional genomics work on the molecular basis of drought induced CAM-cycling photosynthesis. These pathways may be useful for engineering improved water use efficiency and drought tolerance into C3 or C4 crop plants.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec009" name="sec009" data-toc="sec009" class="link-target" title="Methods"></a><h2>Methods</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec010" name="sec010" class="link-target" title="Plant materials"></a>
<h3>Plant materials</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p><em>Sedum album</em> plants were vegetatively propagated via cuttings in growth chambers under 12-hour photoperiod with day/night temperatures of 24°C and 20°C respectively. The light period started at 8am (ZT0) and ended at 8pm (ZT12). To induce CAM activity, plants were subjected to moderate drought by withholding water for 14 days. Well-watered (C3) control plants were watered every 2 days for the duration of the experiment. For the 24-hour diurnal timecourse experiments, leaves from 5 pots of randomly sampled plants were pooled for each replicate and three biological replicates were collected for each timepoint. The well-watered (C3) 6am (ZT22) and drought (CAM) 8am (ZT0) samples had two and one replicates respectively due to loss of samples. Samples were collected every 2 hours over the 24-hour experiment. All tissues were frozen into liquid nitrogen immediately and stored at -80C.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec011" name="sec011" class="link-target" title="Carbohydrate and titratable acid assays"></a>
<h3>Carbohydrate and titratable acid assays</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Carbohydrate and titratable acidity data was collected for each of the samples used in the C3 and CAM timecourse experiment. Carbohydrate and titratable acidity measurements were taken from the same tissue samples used for RNAseq experiment. Frozen leaf tissue (0.1–0.5g) was ground, weighed and transferred into 1.5mL tubes. 500uL of 3.5% perchloric acid was added to the leaf tissue and mixed by vortexing. The resulting supernatant (perchloric acid extract) was neutralized to pH 7.0 using neutralizing buffer (2M KOH, 150mM Hepes, and 10mM KCl). The samples were then frozen to precipitate salts, centrifuged, and the supernatant was transferred to a new 1.5mL tube for carbohydrate assays. Sucrose, glucose, fructose and fructose-6-phosphate, β-maltose, and glucose-6-phosphate were measured. The pellet was resuspended in 1mL of 80% ethanol and mixed by vortexing followed by two rounds of washing. The pellet was air-dried for 30 min, resuspensed in 200mM KOH and incubated at 95°C for 30 min. 1M acetic acid was used to adjust the pH to 5.0 and 5 ul of an enzyme cocktail containing 5 units α-amylase (E-ANAAM Megazyme, Bray, Wicklow, Ireland) and 6.6 units amylogucosidase (E-AMGDF Megazyme) was added to each tube and incubated at room temperature for 24 hours. The resulting supernatant containing glucose was transferred to a new tube for starch content measurement using the glucose assay.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p2" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Glucose content was measured using a 96 well plate in a Filter Max F5 plate reader (MDS Analytical Technologies, Sunnyvale CA, USA) at 340 nm with an NADP(H)-linked assay. Wells were filled with 200 μl of 150 mM Hepes buffer pH 7.2 containing 15 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 3 mM EDTA, 500 nmol NADP, 500 nmol ATP and 0.4 units glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase (G8529 Sigma St. Louis MO, USA). Five μl of sample was added to each well and the reaction was started by adding 0.5 units of hexokinase (H4502 Sigma). Absolute glucose content was determined using an extinction coefficient of 6220 L mol<sup>-1</sup> cm<sup>-1</sup> for NADPH at 340 nm[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref066" class="ref-tip">66</a>]. Sucrose content was assayed as reported above with 20U of invertase (Sigma-aldrich, I4505) and 0.5U of hexokinase. Other carbohydrates (β-maltose, glucose-6-phosphate, glucose, fructose and fructose-6-phosphate) were measured as previously reported by Weise et al. [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref063" class="ref-tip">63</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref067" class="ref-tip">67</a>]. All enzymes used were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich and 4 unit of maltose phosphorylase was used.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p3" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>To measure titratable acidity, ~0.3g of fresh ground tissue was mixed with 3mL of 80% ethanol and boiled at 80°C for 60 min. The supernatant was cooled to room temperature and titrated with 0.1N sodium hydroxide until an endpoint pH of 8.3. Titratable acid (in μ Eq per gram of fresh weight) was calculated as volume of 0.1N sodium hydroxide X 0.1 X 1000 / Fresh weight (gram).</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec012" name="sec012" class="link-target" title="Stomatal aperture measurements"></a>
<h3>Stomatal aperture measurements</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Epidermal peels from well-watered (C3) and drought stressed (CAM) <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> leaves were prepared according to Wu et al. [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref068" class="ref-tip">68</a>]. Microscope slides were visualized using a Nikon Eclipse Ni-Upright microscope with 40X differential interference contrast objective lens and images were captured with Nikon DS-Fi3 camera. Stomatal width and length was measured using program NIS-Elements and recorded in an Excel file. A minimum of 10 individual stoma from 5 leaves were examined for each time point (ZT06 and ZT22) and condition (C3 and CAM-cycling). A Student’s t-test was performed to test for significant changes in stomatal aperture.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec013" name="sec013" class="link-target" title="DNA isolation and sequencing"></a>
<h3>DNA isolation and sequencing</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>High molecular weight (HMW) genomic DNA was isolated from well-watered <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> leaf tissue for PacBio and Illumina sequencing. HMW gDNA was isolated using a modified nuclei preparation [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref069" class="ref-tip">69</a>] followed by phenol chloroform purification to remove residual contaminants. PacBio libraries size selected for 25kb fragments on the BluePippen system (Sage Science) and purified using AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter). The 25kb PacBio libraries were sequenced on a PacBio RSII system with P6C4 chemistry. In total, 4 million PacBio reads were sequenced, collectively spanning 33.7 Gb or 55x genome coverage. Illumina DNAseq libraries were constructed from the same batch of HMW gDNA using the KAPA HyperPrep Kit (Kapa Biosystems) followed by sequencing on an Illumina HiSeq4000 under paired end mode (150 bp). In total, 38 Gb of Illumina data was generated for error correction, representing ~62x coverage.</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec014" name="sec014" class="link-target" title="RNA extraction and RNAseq library construction"></a>
<h3>RNA extraction and RNAseq library construction</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>RNA was extracted using the Omega Biotek E.Z.N.A. Plant RNA kit according to the manufacturer’s protocol. RNA quality was examined on a 1% agarose gel and RNA concentration was quantified using the Qubit RNA HS assay kit (Invitrogen, USA). 2μg of total RNA was used to construct stranded RNAseq library using the Illumina TruSeq stranded total RNA LT sample prep kit (RS-122-2401 and RS-122-2402). Multiplexed libraries were pooled and sequenced on HiSeq4000 using paired end 150nt mode.</p>
</div>

<div id="section6" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec015" name="sec015" class="link-target" title="Genome assembly and annotation"></a>
<h3>Genome assembly and annotation</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Given the complex polyploidy of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, several long read assembly algorithms were tested for their ability to resolve and collapse homeologous regions. Raw PacBio reads were error corrected using Falcon (V0.2.2)[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref031" class="ref-tip">31</a>] and Canu (V1.4) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref070" class="ref-tip">70</a>]. Parameters for Falcon were left as default. The following parameters for Canu were modified: minReadLength = 2000, GenomeSize = 612Mb, minOverlapLength = 1000. The Canu assembly accurately separated the homeologous regions and produced an assembly of 627 Mb across 15,256 contigs with an N50 of 47kb. The Canu assembly graph was visualized in Bandage [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref071" class="ref-tip">71</a>] and most of the nodes (contigs) were highly interconnected. The assembly graph complexity is likely caused by a combination of polyploidy and heterozygosity. This Canu based assembly is referred as tetraploid genome version of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> and named as Sedum V2 genome. Homeologous regions in the Falcon based assembly were highly collapsed and the total assembly size was 302 Mb with 6,038 contigs and an N50 of 93kb. This assembly is about half of the estimated genome size, and represents a diploidized version of the tetraploid <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> genome. We named this Falcon version as diploid genome version and designated as Sedum album V3 genome. We assessed the completeness of the tetraploid and diploidized assemblies using the benchmarking universal single-copy orthologs (BUSCO; v.2) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref072" class="ref-tip">72</a>] with the plant specific dataset (embryophyta_odb9). ~97% of the 1,440 plant specific genes were identified in both <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> assemblies, supporting they contained an accurate representation of the gene space.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p2" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Contigs from the Falcon and Canu assemblies were polished to remove residual errors with Pilon (V1.22) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref033" class="ref-tip">33</a>] using 62x coverage of Illumina pared-end 150 bp data. Illumina reads were quality-trimmed using Trimmomatic [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref073" class="ref-tip">73</a>] followed by aligning to the assembly using bowtie2 (V2.3.0)[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref074" class="ref-tip">74</a>] with default parameters. The total alignment rate of Illumina data was 92.3% for the Falcon assembly and 93.1% for Canu, suggesting both were largely complete. Pilon parameters were modified as followed and all others were left as default:—flank 7,—K 49, and—mindepth 15. Pilon was run four times to remove any residual errors.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p3" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>The <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> diploid and tetraploid assemblies were annotated using the MAKER-P pipeline [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref034" class="ref-tip">34</a>]. A custom library of long terminal repeat [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref075" class="ref-tip">75</a>] retrotransposons was constructed for repeat masking. LTR harvest (genome tools V1.5.8) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref076" class="ref-tip">76</a>] and LTR Finder (v1.07) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref077" class="ref-tip">77</a>] were used to predict putative LTRs and this candidate list was refined using LTR retriever (v1.8.0) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref078" class="ref-tip">78</a>]. Parameters for LTR harvest and LTR finder were assigned based on suggestions from the LTR retriever package. This high quality library was used as input for RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref079" class="ref-tip">79</a>] with implementation in the MAKER pipeline. RNAseq data from the C3 and CAM-cycling timecourses was used as transcript evidence. Representative transcripts from the RNAseq data were assembled using Trinity [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref080" class="ref-tip">80</a>] with default parameters. Protein sequences from Arabidopsis [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref081" class="ref-tip">81</a>] and the UniprotKB plant databases [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref082" class="ref-tip">82</a>] were used as protein evidence. <em>Ab initio</em> gene prediction was done using SNAP [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref083" class="ref-tip">83</a>] and Augustus (3.0.2) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref084" class="ref-tip">84</a>] with two rounds of training. Transposable element derived gene models were filtered using a library of representative transposases.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p4" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>To identify homologs between the diploidized and tetraploid genome assemblies, a reciprocal blast approach was used. First, blastp was run using the tetraploid gene model sequences as query against the diploid gene models with an e-value cutoff of 0.001, maximum target sequence of 1, HSP ≥ 100, protein similarity ≥60%, and match length &gt; 50 amino acids. A reciprocal blastp search was performed with the reversed query and database using the same parameters except without maximum target sequence option. Only gene pairs retained in both blastp results are identified as homologs between the two genome versions.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p5" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Expression profiles between the tetraploid and diploid assemblies were compared to assess if the diploid assembly contained a representative gene set for downstream analyses. Expression levels (in TPM) of the RNA-seq samples were quantified using Kallisto v0.43.0[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref085" class="ref-tip">85</a>] with the tetraploid and diploid gene model sets. Expression values from all timepoints collected under C3 or CAM were averaged and plotted for homologous gene pairs in the diploid and tetraploid genome assemblies. Pearson correlation of all homologous gene pairs were calculated to assess the transcript expression similarity between two genomes. Based on the high expression correlation, the diploid version of the assembly and annotation was used for downstream analyses.</p>
</div>

<div id="section7" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec016" name="sec016" class="link-target" title="RNAseq expression and differential expression analysis"></a>
<h3>RNAseq expression and differential expression analysis</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec7.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec7.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Paired end raw reads were trimmed using Trimmomatic v0.33 [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref073" class="ref-tip">73</a>] to remove adapters and low quality bases. Quality trimmed reads were pseudo-aligned to the <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> gene models to quantify expression using Kallisto v0.43.0[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref085" class="ref-tip">85</a>]. Default parameters were used for Kallisto and 100 bootstraps were run per sample. Transcript expression was quantified in transcripts per million (TPM) and an averaged TPM from the three replicates was used for gene co-expression and single gene analyses. Differential expressed genes of each timepoint between C3 and CAM samples are identified using R program sleuth. Likelihood ratio test and Wald test were used and only genes with q-value &lt; 0.05 and b-value &gt; |1| in both tests were categorized as differentially expressed (DE) gene.</p>
</div>

<div id="section8" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec017" name="sec017" class="link-target" title="Gene co-expression and gene interaction networks and circadian rhythm analysis"></a>
<h3>Gene co-expression and gene interaction networks and circadian rhythm analysis</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The timecourse RNAseq data was clustered into gene co-expression networks using the R package WGCNA [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref038" class="ref-tip">38</a>]. Prior to network construction, genes were filtered based on TPM and any gene with total TPM &lt; 5 across all samples or 25% of datapoints have zero expression was removed. In total, 29,372 genes were used to construct separate networks for the well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM-cycling) timecourses. Parameters for the C3 network were as follows: power = 7, networkType =“signed", corType = "bicor", maxPOutliers = 0.05, TOMType = "signed", deepSplit = 3, mergeCutHeight = 0.01. For the CAM-cycling network, the following parameters were used: power = 10, networkType = "signed", corType = "bicor", maxPOutliers = 0.05, TOMType = "signed", deepSplit = 3, mergeCutHeight = 0.15.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p2" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The JTK_CYCLE program [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref044" class="ref-tip">44</a>] was used to detect rhythmic expression patterns of genes across the two timecourses. JTK_CYCLE was implemented in the R package MetaCycle (v. 1.0.0) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref086" class="ref-tip">86</a>]. The 29,372 genes used to construct co-expression networks were tested for rhythmicity under the C3 and CAM-cycling conditions. Genes with Bonferroni adjusted <em>p</em>-value &lt; 0.05 were classified as cycling and changes in amplitude were assessed by subtracting the amplitude under C3 from that for CAM-cycling. Gene with amplitude difference less than 3 was categorized as having no changes between the two conditions. The same 29,372 genes used in WGCNA and JTK_CYCLE analysis were used for gene interaction network construction using default setting of program CMIP [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref087" class="ref-tip">87</a>, <a href="#pgen.1008209.ref088" class="ref-tip">88</a>]. The threshold for the C3 and CAM networks was 0.47 and 0.52, respectively. The gene interaction pairs between C3 and CAM networks were compared. Gene interacting pairs that were found in the C3 network but not in the CAM network were classified as “repressor” and gene interactions that were predicted in the CAM network but not in C3 were categorized as “activator”.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p3" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>For circadian rhythmicity, JTK_CYCLE analysis across the four species (<em>S</em>. <em>album</em>, <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em>, <em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em> and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em>) was performed as stated above. The <em>B</em>. <em>rapa</em> well-watered and drought datasets, were downloaded from NCBI GEO GSE90841 and only the second 24 hours of data from the well-watered and drought conditions were used for analysis. For <em>A</em>. <em>comosus</em>, the leaf green tip and white base datasets published in [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref028" class="ref-tip">28</a>] were used and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> leaf timecourse dataset were downloaded from the NCBI SRA database (BioSample SAMN07453940-SAMN07453987). An expression matrix table was provided by Dr. Xiaohan Yang (Oak Ridge National Laboratory, USA). These expression datasets were filtered using the same criteria as <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> as stated. For phase enrichment analysis, Bonferroni adjusted <em>p</em>-value of each phase (0–23) was calculated using R program and phases with an adjusted <em>p</em>-value ≤ 0.05 was classified as enriched.</p>
</div>

<div id="section9" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec018" name="sec018" class="link-target" title="GO enrichment analysis"></a>
<h3>GO enrichment analysis</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Gene Ontology (GO) terms of <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> protein sequences were annotated using InterProScan 5 [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref089" class="ref-tip">89</a>]. GO terms for genes without InterProScan annotations were inferred using corresponding GO term from the top BLAST hit to Arabidopsis orthologs. The GO terms from both methods were merged and 33,362 genes had at least one annotated GO term. GO enrichment analysis was performed using the R package TopGO [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref090" class="ref-tip">90</a>] with Fisher’s exact test and Bonferroni adjusted <em>p</em>-values.</p>
</div>

<div id="section10" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec019" name="sec019" class="link-target" title="Gene family and phylogenetic analyses"></a>
<h3>Gene family and phylogenetic analyses</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>OrthoFinder (v1.1.9) [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref091" class="ref-tip">91</a>] was used to identify gene families between sequenced CAM and representative C3 and C4 species. The CAM species <em>Sedum album</em>, <em>Phalaenopsis equestris</em>, <em>Dendrobium catenatum</em>, <em>Dendrobium officinale</em>, <em>Apostasia shenzhenica</em>, <em>Ananas comosus</em>, and <em>Kalanchoe fedtschenkoi</em>, C3 species <em>Brassica rapa</em>, <em>Oryza sativa</em>, <em>Solanum lycopersicum</em>, <em>Populus trichocarpa</em> and <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> and C4 species <em>Sorghum bicolor</em>, <em>Setaria italica</em> were used for orthogroup identification. Protein sequences from each species were downloaded from Phytozome V12. Orthofinder was run using default parameters. Only orthologs from the CAM species pineapple and <em>K</em>. <em>fedtschenkoi</em> were included in downstream analyses as timecourse data for orchid (<em>Phalaenopsis equestris</em>) is currently unavailable.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p2" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>To assess the phylogenetic relationships between orthologs, protein sequences of genes within the same orthogroup identified by OrthoFinder were extracted and aligned with MUSCLE (v.3.8.31)[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref092" class="ref-tip">92</a>] and maximum likelihood phylogenetic trees were built using RAxML (v8.2.10)[<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref093" class="ref-tip">93</a>] with bootstraps of 100 and the–m PROTGAMMAJTT flag.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p3" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Orthogroups were further analyzed to identify expansions and contractions unique to <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> compared to other CAM as well as C3 and C4 species. The number of genes for each species was normalized by the total number of genes in the genome to account for differences in gene number and ploidy between species, as previously described [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref094" class="ref-tip">94</a>]. Orthogroups were classified as contracted with a ratio of less than 0.2 in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> compared to other species, and orthogroups with a ratio greater than 3 in <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> compared to other species were classified as expanded.</p>
</div>

<div id="section11" class="section toc-section"><a id="sec020" name="sec020" class="link-target" title="cis-element analysis"></a>
<h3>cis-element analysis</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec11.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec11.p1" class="link-target"></a><p><em>cis</em>-element analysis was carried out as previously described [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref021" class="ref-tip">21</a>]. The <em>S</em>. <em>album</em> promoters (500, 1000, 2000 bp) were parsed using the gene models. Gene lists were generated for each phase as called by JTK_CYCLE with a cycling significance cut-off of 0.05 and for specific conditions: CAM only cycling, C3 only cycling, C3 and CAM cycling with equal expression, C3 and CAM cycling with greater C3 expression, C3 and CAM cycling with greater CAM expression, Overrepresentation of <em>cis</em>-element was calculated for all of 3–8 mers using the ELEMENT program [<a href="#pgen.1008209.ref095" class="ref-tip">95</a>] with a <em>p</em>-value cut off of 0.05.</p>
</div>
</div>

</div><div class="supplementary-material"><a name="pgen.1008209.s011" id="pgen.1008209.s011" class="link-target"></a><h3 class="siTitle title-small"><a href="article/file?type=supplementary&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209.s011">S11 Fig. </a>Expression of genes involved in starch metabolism pathway under well-watered (C3) and drought (CAM-cycling) conditions.</h3><a id="article1.body1.sec5.supplementary-material11.caption1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec5.supplementary-material11.caption1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p class="preSiDOI">Expression of genes involved in starch synthesis, cytosolic bypass, G6P shunt and starch degradation pathways are plotted.   var articleDoi = "10.1371/journal.pgen.1008209";
  $.ajax({
    url: "https://collections.plos.org/wp-json/collections-api/contains",
    data: {id: articleDoi},
    context: document.body,
    timeout: 10000,
    dataType: "json",
    success: function (result, textStatus, xOptions) {
      if (result.response == "success" && result.data.collections.length > 0) {
        var articleCollectionsTemplate = _.template($('#articleCollectionsTemplate').html());
        var articleCollectionsHTML = articleCollectionsTemplate(result.data)
        $('.collections-aside-container').append(articleCollectionsHTML);
      }
    }
  });
